</div>
</td>

<p><b>Nanotechnology</b> ("<b>nanotech</b>") is manipulation of matter on an <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atomic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molecular" class="mw-redirect" title="Molecular">molecular</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Supramolecular_complex" class="mw-redirect" title="Supramolecular complex">supramolecular</a> scale. The earliest, widespread description of nanotechnology<sup id="cite_ref-Engines1_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Engines1-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nanotsystems_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nanotsystems-2">[2]</a></sup> referred to the particular technological goal of precisely manipulating atoms and molecules for fabrication of macroscale products, also now referred to as <a href="/wiki/Molecular_nanotechnology" title="Molecular nanotechnology">molecular nanotechnology</a>. A more generalized description of nanotechnology was subsequently established by the <a href="/wiki/National_Nanotechnology_Initiative" title="National Nanotechnology Initiative">National Nanotechnology Initiative</a>, which defines nanotechnology as the manipulation of matter with at least one dimension sized from 1 to 100 <a href="/wiki/Nanometers" class="mw-redirect" title="Nanometers">nanometers</a>. This definition reflects the fact that <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanical</a> effects are important at this <a href="/wiki/Quantum_realm" title="Quantum realm">quantum-realm</a> scale, and so the definition shifted from a particular technological goal to a research category inclusive of all types of research and technologies that deal with the special properties of matter which occur below the given size threshold. It is therefore common to see the plural form "nanotechnologies" as well as "nanoscale technologies" to refer to the broad range of research and applications whose common trait is size. Because of the variety of potential applications (including industrial and military), governments have invested billions of dollars in nanotechnology research. Until 2012, through its National Nanotechnology Initiative, the USA has invested 3.7 billion dollars, the European Union has invested 1.2 billion and Japan 750 million dollars.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nanotechnology as defined by size is naturally very broad, including fields of science as diverse as <a href="/wiki/Surface_science" title="Surface science">surface science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic chemistry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molecular_biology" title="Molecular biology">molecular biology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor_physics" class="mw-redirect" title="Semiconductor physics">semiconductor physics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Microfabrication" title="Microfabrication">microfabrication</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molecular_engineering" title="Molecular engineering">molecular engineering</a>, etc.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The associated research and applications are equally diverse, ranging from extensions of conventional <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor_device" title="Semiconductor device">device physics</a> to completely new approaches based upon <a href="/wiki/Molecular_self-assembly" title="Molecular self-assembly">molecular self-assembly</a>, from developing <a href="/wiki/Nanomaterial" class="mw-redirect" title="Nanomaterial">new materials</a> with dimensions on the nanoscale to <a href="/wiki/Molecular_nanotechnology" title="Molecular nanotechnology">direct control of matter on the atomic scale</a>.</p>
<p>Scientists currently debate the future <a href="/wiki/Implications_of_nanotechnology" class="mw-redirect" title="Implications of nanotechnology">implications of nanotechnology</a>. Nanotechnology may be able to create many new materials and devices with a vast range of <a href="/wiki/List_of_nanotechnology_applications" class="mw-redirect" title="List of nanotechnology applications">applications</a>, such as in <a href="/wiki/Nanomedicine" title="Nanomedicine">nanomedicine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nanoelectronics" title="Nanoelectronics">nanoelectronics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biomaterial" title="Biomaterial">biomaterials</a> energy production, and consumer products. On the other hand, nanotechnology raises many of the same issues as any new technology, including concerns about the <a href="/wiki/Nanotoxicology" title="Nanotoxicology">toxicity</a> and environmental impact of nanomaterials,<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> and their potential effects on global economics, as well as speculation about various <a href="/wiki/Grey_goo" title="Grey goo">doomsday scenarios</a>. These concerns have led to a debate among advocacy groups and governments on whether special <a href="/wiki/Regulation_of_nanotechnology" title="Regulation of nanotechnology">regulation of nanotechnology</a> is warranted.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_nanotechnology" title="History of nanotechnology">History of nanotechnology</a></div>
<p>The concepts that seeded nanotechnology were first discussed in 1959 by renowned physicist <a href="/wiki/Richard_Feynman" title="Richard Feynman">Richard Feynman</a> in his talk <i><a href="/wiki/There%27s_Plenty_of_Room_at_the_Bottom" title="There's Plenty of Room at the Bottom">There's Plenty of Room at the Bottom</a></i>, in which he described the possibility of synthesis via direct manipulation of atoms. The term "nano-technology" was first used by <a href="/wiki/Norio_Taniguchi" title="Norio Taniguchi">Norio Taniguchi</a> in 1974, though it was not widely known.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Comparison_of_nanomaterials_sizes.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Comparison_of_nanomaterials_sizes.jpg/400px-Comparison_of_nanomaterials_sizes.jpg" width="400" height="369" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Comparison_of_nanomaterials_sizes.jpg/600px-Comparison_of_nanomaterials_sizes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Comparison_of_nanomaterials_sizes.jpg/800px-Comparison_of_nanomaterials_sizes.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7824" data-file-height="7216" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Comparison_of_nanomaterials_sizes.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Comparison of Nanomaterials Sizes</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Inspired by Feynman's concepts, <a href="/wiki/K._Eric_Drexler" title="K. Eric Drexler">K. Eric Drexler</a> used the term "nanotechnology" in his 1986 book <i><a href="/wiki/Engines_of_creation" class="mw-redirect" title="Engines of creation">Engines of Creation: The Coming Era of Nanotechnology</a></i>, which proposed the idea of a nanoscale "assembler" which would be able to build a copy of itself and of other items of arbitrary complexity with atomic control. Also in 1986, Drexler co-founded <a href="/wiki/The_Foresight_Institute" class="mw-redirect" title="The Foresight Institute">The Foresight Institute</a> (with which he is no longer affiliated) to help increase public awareness and understanding of nanotechnology concepts and implications.</p>
<p>Thus, emergence of nanotechnology as a field in the 1980s occurred through convergence of Drexler's theoretical and public work, which developed and popularized a conceptual framework for nanotechnology, and high-visibility experimental advances that drew additional wide-scale attention to the prospects of atomic control of matter. In the 1980s, two major breakthroughs sparked the growth of nanotechnology in modern era.</p>
<p>First, the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Scanning_tunneling_microscope" title="Scanning tunneling microscope">scanning tunneling microscope</a> in 1981 which provided unprecedented visualization of individual atoms and bonds, and was successfully used to manipulate individual atoms in 1989. The microscope's developers <a href="/wiki/Gerd_Binnig" title="Gerd Binnig">Gerd Binnig</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Rohrer" title="Heinrich Rohrer">Heinrich Rohrer</a> at <a href="/wiki/IBM_Zurich_Research_Laboratory" title="IBM Zurich Research Laboratory">IBM Zurich Research Laboratory</a> received a <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physics" title="Nobel Prize in Physics">Nobel Prize in Physics</a> in 1986.<sup id="cite_ref-Binnig_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Binnig-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Binnig, <a href="/wiki/Calvin_F._Quate" class="mw-redirect" title="Calvin F. Quate">Quate</a> and Gerber also invented the analogous <a href="/wiki/Atomic_force_microscope" class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic force microscope">atomic force microscope</a> that year.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;"><a href="/wiki/File:C60a.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/C60a.png/175px-C60a.png" width="175" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/C60a.png/263px-C60a.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/C60a.png/350px-C60a.png 2x" data-file-width="627" data-file-height="614" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:C60a.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Buckminsterfullerene C<sub>60</sub>, also known as the <a href="/wiki/Buckyball_(molecule)" class="mw-redirect" title="Buckyball (molecule)">buckyball</a>, is a representative member of the <a href="/wiki/Allotropes_of_carbon" title="Allotropes of carbon">carbon structures</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Fullerene" title="Fullerene">fullerenes</a>. Members of the fullerene family are a major subject of research falling under the nanotechnology umbrella.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Second, <a href="/wiki/Fullerenes" class="mw-redirect" title="Fullerenes">Fullerenes</a> were discovered in 1985 by <a href="/wiki/Harry_Kroto" title="Harry Kroto">Harry Kroto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Smalley" title="Richard Smalley">Richard Smalley</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Curl" title="Robert Curl">Robert Curl</a>, who together won the 1996 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize in Chemistry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> C<sub>60</sub> was not initially described as nanotechnology; the term was used regarding subsequent work with related <a href="/wiki/Graphene" title="Graphene">graphene</a> tubes (called <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nanotube" title="Carbon nanotube">carbon nanotubes</a> and sometimes called Bucky tubes) which suggested potential applications for nanoscale electronics and devices.</p>
<p>In the early 2000s, the field garnered increased scientific, political, and commercial attention that led to both controversy and progress. Controversies emerged regarding the definitions and potential implications of nanotechnologies, exemplified by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a>'s report on nanotechnology.<sup id="cite_ref-royalsociety_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-royalsociety-10">[10]</a></sup> Challenges were raised regarding the feasibility of applications envisioned by advocates of molecular nanotechnology, which culminated in a public debate between Drexler and Smalley in 2001 and 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-counterpoint_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-counterpoint-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Meanwhile, commercialization of products based on advancements in nanoscale technologies began emerging. These products are limited to bulk applications of <a href="/wiki/Nanomaterials" title="Nanomaterials">nanomaterials</a> and do not involve atomic control of matter. Some examples include the <a href="/wiki/Silver_Nano" title="Silver Nano">Silver Nano</a> platform for using <a href="/wiki/Silver_nanoparticles" class="mw-redirect" title="Silver nanoparticles">silver nanoparticles</a> as an antibacterial agent, <a href="/wiki/Nanoparticle" title="Nanoparticle">nanoparticle</a>-based transparent sunscreens, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_fibers" title="Carbon fibers">carbon fiber</a> strengthening using silica nanoparticles, and carbon nanotubes for stain-resistant textiles.<sup id="cite_ref-americanelements_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-americanelements-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-emergingnano_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emergingnano-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Governments moved to promote and <a href="/wiki/Funding_of_science" title="Funding of science">fund research</a> into nanotechnology, such as in the U.S. with the <a href="/wiki/National_Nanotechnology_Initiative" title="National Nanotechnology Initiative">National Nanotechnology Initiative</a>, which formalized a size-based definition of nanotechnology and established funding for research on the nanoscale, and in Europe via the European <a href="/wiki/Framework_Programmes_for_Research_and_Technological_Development" title="Framework Programmes for Research and Technological Development">Framework Programmes for Research and Technological Development</a>.</p>
<p>By the mid-2000s new and serious scientific attention began to flourish. Projects emerged to produce nanotechnology roadmaps<sup id="cite_ref-PNRoadmap_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PNRoadmap-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NASAroadmap_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASAroadmap-15">[15]</a></sup> which center on atomically precise manipulation of matter and discuss existing and projected capabilities, goals, and applications.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fundamental_concepts">Fundamental concepts</span></h2>
<p>Nanotechnology is the engineering of functional systems at the molecular scale. This covers both current work and concepts that are more advanced. In its original sense, nanotechnology refers to the projected ability to construct items from the bottom up, using techniques and tools being developed today to make complete, high performance products.</p>
<p>One <a href="/wiki/Nanometer" class="mw-redirect" title="Nanometer">nanometer</a> (nm) is one billionth, or 10<sup>−9</sup>, of a meter. By comparison, typical carbon-carbon <a href="/wiki/Bond_length" title="Bond length">bond lengths</a>, or the spacing between these <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecule</a>, are in the range <span class="nowrap">0.12–0.15 nm</span>, and a <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> double-helix has a diameter around 2&#160;nm. On the other hand, the smallest <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cellular</a> life-forms, the bacteria of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Mycoplasma" title="Mycoplasma">Mycoplasma</a></i>, are around 200&#160;nm in length. By convention, nanotechnology is taken as the scale range <span class="nowrap">1 to 100 nm</span> following the definition used by the National Nanotechnology Initiative in the US. The lower limit is set by the size of atoms (hydrogen has the smallest atoms, which are approximately a quarter of a nm diameter) since nanotechnology must build its devices from atoms and molecules. The upper limit is more or less arbitrary but is around the size below which phenomena not observed in larger structures start to become apparent and can be made use of in the nano device.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> These new phenomena make nanotechnology distinct from devices which are merely miniaturised versions of an equivalent <a href="/wiki/Macroscopic_scale" title="Macroscopic scale">macroscopic</a> device; such devices are on a larger scale and come under the description of <a href="/wiki/Microtechnology" title="Microtechnology">microtechnology</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>To put that scale in another context, the comparative size of a nanometer to a meter is the same as that of a marble to the size of the earth.<sup id="cite_ref-NationalG_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NationalG-18">[18]</a></sup> Or another way of putting it: a nanometer is the amount an average man's beard grows in the time it takes him to raise the razor to his face.<sup id="cite_ref-NationalG_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NationalG-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>Two main approaches are used in nanotechnology. In the "bottom-up" approach, materials and devices are built from molecular components which <a href="/wiki/Self-assembly" title="Self-assembly">assemble themselves</a> chemically by principles of <a href="/wiki/Molecular_recognition" title="Molecular recognition">molecular recognition</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-19">[19]</a></sup> In the "top-down" approach, nano-objects are constructed from larger entities without atomic-level control.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>Areas of physics such as <a href="/wiki/Nanoelectronics" title="Nanoelectronics">nanoelectronics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nanomechanics" title="Nanomechanics">nanomechanics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nanophotonics" title="Nanophotonics">nanophotonics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nanoionics" title="Nanoionics">nanoionics</a> have evolved during the last few decades to provide a basic scientific foundation of nanotechnology.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Larger_to_smaller:_a_materials_perspective">Larger to smaller: a materials perspective</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Atomic_resolution_Au100.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Atomic_resolution_Au100.JPG/220px-Atomic_resolution_Au100.JPG" width="220" height="211" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Atomic_resolution_Au100.JPG/330px-Atomic_resolution_Au100.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Atomic_resolution_Au100.JPG/440px-Atomic_resolution_Au100.JPG 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Atomic_resolution_Au100.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Image of <a href="/wiki/Surface_reconstruction" title="Surface reconstruction">reconstruction</a> on a clean <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">Gold</a>(<a href="/wiki/Miller_index" title="Miller index">100</a>) surface, as visualized using <a href="/wiki/Scanning_tunneling_microscopy" class="mw-redirect" title="Scanning tunneling microscopy">scanning tunneling microscopy</a>. The positions of the individual atoms composing the surface are visible.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nanomaterials" title="Nanomaterials">Nanomaterials</a></div>
<p>Several phenomena become pronounced as the size of the system decreases. These include <a href="/wiki/Statistical_mechanics" title="Statistical mechanics">statistical mechanical</a> effects, as well as <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanical</a> effects, for example the "<a href="/wiki/Quantum" title="Quantum">quantum</a> size effect" where the electronic properties of solids are altered with great reductions in particle size. This effect does not come into play by going from macro to micro dimensions. However, quantum effects can become significant when the nanometer size range is reached, typically at distances of 100 nanometers or less, the so-called <a href="/wiki/Quantum_realm" title="Quantum realm">quantum realm</a>. Additionally, a number of physical (mechanical, electrical, optical, etc.) properties change when compared to macroscopic systems. One example is the increase in surface area to volume ratio altering mechanical, thermal and catalytic properties of materials. Diffusion and reactions at nanoscale, nanostructures materials and nanodevices with fast ion transport are generally referred to nanoionics. <i>Mechanical</i> properties of nanosystems are of interest in the nanomechanics research. The catalytic activity of nanomaterials also opens potential risks in their interaction with <a href="/wiki/Biomaterial" title="Biomaterial">biomaterials</a>.</p>
<p>Materials reduced to the nanoscale can show different properties compared to what they exhibit on a macroscale, enabling unique applications. For instance, opaque substances can become transparent (copper); stable materials can turn combustible (aluminium); insoluble materials may become soluble (gold). A material such as gold, which is chemically inert at normal scales, can serve as a potent chemical <a href="/wiki/Catalyst" class="mw-redirect" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a> at nanoscales. Much of the fascination with nanotechnology stems from these quantum and surface phenomena that matter exhibits at the nanoscale.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Simple_to_complex:_a_molecular_perspective">Simple to complex: a molecular perspective</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Molecular_self-assembly" title="Molecular self-assembly">Molecular self-assembly</a></div>
<p>Modern <a href="/wiki/Chemical_synthesis" title="Chemical synthesis">synthetic chemistry</a> has reached the point where it is possible to prepare small molecules to almost any structure. These methods are used today to manufacture a wide variety of useful chemicals such as <a href="/wiki/Drug" title="Drug">pharmaceuticals</a> or commercial <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymers</a>. This ability raises the question of extending this kind of control to the next-larger level, seeking methods to assemble these single molecules into <a href="/wiki/Supramolecular_assembly" title="Supramolecular assembly">supramolecular assemblies</a> consisting of many molecules arranged in a well defined manner.</p>
<p>These approaches utilize the concepts of molecular self-assembly and/or <a href="/wiki/Supramolecular_chemistry" title="Supramolecular chemistry">supramolecular chemistry</a> to automatically arrange themselves into some useful conformation through a <a href="/wiki/Top-down_and_bottom-up#Nanotechnology" class="mw-redirect" title="Top-down and bottom-up">bottom-up</a> approach. The concept of molecular recognition is especially important: molecules can be designed so that a specific configuration or arrangement is favored due to <a href="/wiki/Noncovalent_bonding" class="mw-redirect" title="Noncovalent bonding">non-covalent</a> <a href="/wiki/Intermolecular_force" title="Intermolecular force">intermolecular forces</a>. The Watson–Crick <a href="/wiki/Base_pair" title="Base pair">basepairing</a> rules are a direct result of this, as is the specificity of an <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a> being targeted to a single <a href="/wiki/Substrate_(biochemistry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Substrate (biochemistry)">substrate</a>, or the specific <a href="/wiki/Protein_folding" title="Protein folding">folding of the protein</a> itself. Thus, two or more components can be designed to be complementary and mutually attractive so that they make a more complex and useful whole.</p>
<p>Such bottom-up approaches should be capable of producing devices in parallel and be much cheaper than top-down methods, but could potentially be overwhelmed as the size and complexity of the desired assembly increases. Most useful structures require complex and thermodynamically unlikely arrangements of atoms. Nevertheless, there are many examples of self-assembly based on molecular recognition in <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, most notably Watson–Crick basepairing and enzyme-substrate interactions. The challenge for nanotechnology is whether these principles can be used to engineer new constructs in addition to natural ones.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Molecular_nanotechnology:_a_long-term_view">Molecular nanotechnology: a long-term view</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Molecular_nanotechnology" title="Molecular nanotechnology">Molecular nanotechnology</a></div>
<p>Molecular nanotechnology, sometimes called molecular manufacturing, describes engineered nanosystems (nanoscale machines) operating on the molecular scale. Molecular nanotechnology is especially associated with the <a href="/wiki/Molecular_assembler" title="Molecular assembler">molecular assembler</a>, a machine that can produce a desired structure or device atom-by-atom using the principles of <a href="/wiki/Mechanosynthesis" title="Mechanosynthesis">mechanosynthesis</a>. Manufacturing in the context of <a href="/wiki/Productive_nanosystems" title="Productive nanosystems">productive nanosystems</a> is not related to, and should be clearly distinguished from, the conventional technologies used to manufacture nanomaterials such as carbon nanotubes and nanoparticles.</p>
<p>When the term "nanotechnology" was independently coined and popularized by <a href="/wiki/Eric_Drexler" class="mw-redirect" title="Eric Drexler">Eric Drexler</a> (who at the time was unaware of an <a href="/wiki/History_of_nanotechnology" title="History of nanotechnology">earlier usage</a> by Norio Taniguchi) it referred to a future manufacturing technology based on <a href="/wiki/Molecular_machine" title="Molecular machine">molecular machine</a> systems. The premise was that molecular scale biological analogies of traditional machine components demonstrated molecular machines were possible: by the countless examples found in biology, it is known that sophisticated, <a href="/wiki/Stochastic" title="Stochastic">stochastically</a> optimised <a href="/wiki/Molecular_machine#Biological" title="Molecular machine">biological machines</a> can be produced.</p>
<p>It is hoped that developments in nanotechnology will make possible their construction by some other means, perhaps using <a href="/wiki/Biomimetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Biomimetic">biomimetic</a> principles. However, Drexler and other researchers<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> have proposed that advanced nanotechnology, although perhaps initially implemented by biomimetic means, ultimately could be based on mechanical engineering principles, namely, a manufacturing technology based on the mechanical functionality of these components (such as gears, bearings, motors, and structural members) that would enable programmable, positional assembly to atomic specification.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The physics and engineering performance of exemplar designs were analyzed in Drexler's book <i>Nanosystems</i>.</p>
<p>In general it is very difficult to assemble devices on the atomic scale, as one has to position atoms on other atoms of comparable size and stickiness. Another view, put forth by Carlo Montemagno,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> is that future nanosystems will be hybrids of silicon technology and biological molecular machines. Richard Smalley argued that mechanosynthesis are impossible due to the difficulties in mechanically manipulating individual molecules.</p>
<p>This led to an exchange of letters in the <a href="/wiki/American_Chemical_Society" title="American Chemical Society">ACS</a> publication <a href="/wiki/Chemical_%26_Engineering_News" title="Chemical &amp; Engineering News">Chemical &amp; Engineering News</a> in 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Though biology clearly demonstrates that molecular machine systems are possible, non-biological molecular machines are today only in their infancy. Leaders in research on non-biological molecular machines are Dr. <a href="/wiki/Alex_Zettl" title="Alex Zettl">Alex Zettl</a> and his colleagues at Lawrence Berkeley Laboratories and UC Berkeley.<a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="http://research.physics.berkeley.edu/zettl/">[1]</a> They have constructed at least three distinct molecular devices whose motion is controlled from the desktop with changing voltage: a nanotube <a href="/wiki/Nanomotor" title="Nanomotor">nanomotor</a>, a molecular actuator,<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> and a nanoelectromechanical relaxation oscillator.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> See <a href="/wiki/Nanotube_nanomotor" class="mw-redirect" title="Nanotube nanomotor">nanotube nanomotor</a> for more examples.</p>
<p>An experiment indicating that positional molecular assembly is possible was performed by Ho and Lee at <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell University</a> in 1999. They used a scanning tunneling microscope to move an individual carbon monoxide molecule (CO) to an individual iron atom (Fe) sitting on a flat silver crystal, and chemically bound the CO to the Fe by applying a voltage.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nanomaterials">Nanomaterials</span></h3>
<p>The nanomaterials field includes subfields which develop or study materials having unique properties arising from their nanoscale dimensions.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Interface_and_colloid_science" title="Interface and colloid science">Interface and colloid science</a> has given rise to many materials which may be useful in nanotechnology, such as carbon nanotubes and other fullerenes, and various nanoparticles and <a href="/wiki/Nanorod" title="Nanorod">nanorods</a>. Nanomaterials with fast ion transport are related also to nanoionics and nanoelectronics.</li>
<li>Nanoscale materials can also be used for bulk applications; most present commercial applications of nanotechnology are of this flavor.</li>
<li>Progress has been made in using these materials for medical applications; see <a href="/wiki/Nanomedicine" title="Nanomedicine">Nanomedicine</a>.</li>
<li>Nanoscale materials such as <a href="/wiki/Nanopillar" title="Nanopillar">nanopillars</a> are sometimes used in <a href="/wiki/Solar_cell" title="Solar cell">solar cells</a> which combats the cost of traditional <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> solar cells.</li>
<li>Development of applications incorporating semiconductor <a href="/wiki/Nanoparticle" title="Nanoparticle">nanoparticles</a> to be used in the next generation of products, such as display technology, lighting, solar cells and biological imaging; see <a href="/wiki/Quantum_dots" class="mw-redirect" title="Quantum dots">quantum dots</a>.</li>
<li>Recent application of <a href="/wiki/Nanomaterials" title="Nanomaterials">nanomaterials</a> include a range of <a href="/wiki/Biomedical" class="mw-redirect" title="Biomedical">biomedical</a> applications, such as <a href="/wiki/Tissue_engineering" title="Tissue engineering">tissue engineering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drug_delivery" title="Drug delivery">drug delivery</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Biosensor" title="Biosensor">biosensors</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bottom-up_approaches">Bottom-up approaches</span></h3>
<p>These seek to arrange smaller components into more complex assemblies.</p>
<ul>
<li>DNA nanotechnology utilizes the specificity of Watson–Crick basepairing to construct well-defined structures out of DNA and other <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acids</a>.</li>
<li>Approaches from the field of "classical" chemical synthesis (Inorganic and <a href="/wiki/Organic_synthesis" title="Organic synthesis">organic synthesis</a>) also aim at designing molecules with well-defined shape (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Bis-peptide" title="Bis-peptide">bis-peptides</a><sup id="cite_ref-Levins_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levins-32">[32]</a></sup>).</li>
<li>More generally, molecular self-assembly seeks to use concepts of supramolecular chemistry, and molecular recognition in particular, to cause single-molecule components to automatically arrange themselves into some useful conformation.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Atomic_force_microscope" class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic force microscope">Atomic force microscope</a> tips can be used as a nanoscale "write head" to deposit a chemical upon a surface in a desired pattern in a process called <a href="/wiki/Dip_pen_nanolithography" class="mw-redirect" title="Dip pen nanolithography">dip pen nanolithography</a>. This technique fits into the larger subfield of <a href="/wiki/Nanolithography" title="Nanolithography">nanolithography</a>.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Top-down_approaches">Top-down approaches</span></h3>
<p>These seek to create smaller devices by using larger ones to direct their assembly.</p>
<ul>
<li>Many technologies that descended from conventional <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor_fabrication" class="mw-redirect" title="Semiconductor fabrication">solid-state silicon methods</a> for fabricating <a href="/wiki/Microprocessor" title="Microprocessor">microprocessors</a> are now capable of creating features smaller than 100&#160;nm, falling under the definition of nanotechnology. <a href="/wiki/Giant_magnetoresistance" title="Giant magnetoresistance">Giant magnetoresistance</a>-based hard drives already on the market fit this description,<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> as do <a href="/wiki/Atomic_layer_deposition" title="Atomic layer deposition">atomic layer deposition</a> (ALD) techniques. <a href="/wiki/Peter_Gr%C3%BCnberg" title="Peter Grünberg">Peter Grünberg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Albert_Fert" title="Albert Fert">Albert Fert</a> received the Nobel Prize in Physics in 2007 for their discovery of Giant magnetoresistance and contributions to the field of spintronics.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></li>
<li>Solid-state techniques can also be used to create devices known as <a href="/wiki/Nanoelectromechanical_systems" title="Nanoelectromechanical systems">nanoelectromechanical systems</a> or NEMS, which are related to <a href="/wiki/Microelectromechanical_systems" title="Microelectromechanical systems">microelectromechanical systems</a> or MEMS.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Focused_ion_beam" title="Focused ion beam">Focused ion beams</a> can directly remove material, or even deposit material when suitable precursor gasses are applied at the same time. For example, this technique is used routinely to create sub-100&#160;nm sections of material for analysis in <a href="/wiki/Transmission_electron_microscopy" title="Transmission electron microscopy">Transmission electron microscopy</a>.</li>
<li>Atomic force microscope tips can be used as a nanoscale "write head" to deposit a resist, which is then followed by an etching process to remove material in a top-down method.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Functional_approaches">Functional approaches</span></h3>
<p>These seek to develop components of a desired functionality without regard to how they might be assembled.</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetic_assembly&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Magnetic assembly (page does not exist)">Magnetic assembly</a> for the synthesis of anisotropic superparamagnetic materials such as recently presented <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_nanochains" title="Magnetic nanochains">magnetic nanochains</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-19">[19]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Molecular_scale_electronics" title="Molecular scale electronics">Molecular scale electronics</a> seeks to develop molecules with useful electronic properties. These could then be used as single-molecule components in a nanoelectronic device.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> For an example see rotaxane.</li>
<li>Synthetic chemical methods can also be used to create <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_molecular_motor" title="Synthetic molecular motor">synthetic molecular motors</a>, such as in a so-called <a href="/wiki/Nanocar" title="Nanocar">nanocar</a>.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biomimetic_approaches">Biomimetic approaches</span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bionics" title="Bionics">Bionics</a> or <a href="/wiki/Biomimicry" class="mw-redirect" title="Biomimicry">biomimicry</a> seeks to apply biological methods and systems found in nature, to the study and design of engineering systems and modern technology. <a href="/wiki/Biomineralization" title="Biomineralization">Biomineralization</a> is one example of the systems studied.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bionanotechnology" class="mw-redirect" title="Bionanotechnology">Bionanotechnology</a> is the use of <a href="/wiki/Biomolecule" title="Biomolecule">biomolecules</a> for applications in nanotechnology, including use of viruses and lipid assemblies.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nanocellulose" title="Nanocellulose">Nanocellulose</a> is a potential bulk-scale application.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Speculative">Speculative</span></h3>
<p>These subfields seek to <a href="/wiki/Futures_studies" title="Futures studies">anticipate</a> what inventions nanotechnology might yield, or attempt to propose an agenda along which inquiry might progress. These often take a big-picture view of nanotechnology, with more emphasis on its societal implications than the details of how such inventions could actually be created.</p>
<ul>
<li>Molecular nanotechnology is a proposed approach which involves manipulating single molecules in finely controlled, deterministic ways. This is more theoretical than the other subfields, and many of its proposed techniques are beyond current capabilities.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nanorobotics" title="Nanorobotics">Nanorobotics</a> centers on self-sufficient machines of some functionality operating at the nanoscale. There are hopes for applying nanorobots in medicine,<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> but it may not be easy to do such a thing because of several drawbacks of such devices.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Nevertheless, progress on innovative materials and methodologies has been demonstrated with some patents granted about new nanomanufacturing devices for future commercial applications, which also progressively helps in the development towards nanorobots with the use of embedded nanobioelectronics concepts.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></li>
<li>Productive nanosystems are "systems of nanosystems" which will be complex nanosystems that produce atomically precise parts for other nanosystems, not necessarily using novel nanoscale-emergent properties, but well-understood fundamentals of manufacturing. Because of the discrete (i.e. atomic) nature of matter and the possibility of exponential growth, this stage is seen as the basis of another industrial revolution. <a href="/wiki/Mihail_Roco" title="Mihail Roco">Mihail Roco</a>, one of the architects of the USA's National Nanotechnology Initiative, has proposed four states of nanotechnology that seem to parallel the technical progress of the Industrial Revolution, progressing from passive nanostructures to active nanodevices to complex <a href="/wiki/Nanomachine" class="mw-redirect" title="Nanomachine">nanomachines</a> and ultimately to productive nanosystems.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Programmable_matter" title="Programmable matter">Programmable matter</a> seeks to design materials whose properties can be easily, reversibly and externally controlled though a fusion of <a href="/wiki/Information_science" title="Information science">information science</a> and <a href="/wiki/Materials_science" title="Materials science">materials science</a>.</li>
<li>Due to the popularity and media exposure of the term nanotechnology, the words <a href="/wiki/Picotechnology" title="Picotechnology">picotechnology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Femtotechnology" title="Femtotechnology">femtotechnology</a> have been coined in analogy to it, although these are only used rarely and informally.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dimensionality_in_nanomaterials">Dimensionality in nanomaterials</span></h3>
<p>Nanomaterials can be classified in 0D, 1D, 2D and 3D <a href="/wiki/Nanomaterials" title="Nanomaterials">nanomaterials</a>. The dimensionality play a major role in determining the characteristic of nanomaterials including <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/physical" class="extiw" title="wikt:physical">physical</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chemical" class="mw-redirect" title="Chemical">chemical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biological" class="mw-redirect" title="Biological">biological</a> characteristics. With the decrease in dimensionality, an increase in surface-to-volume ratio is observed. This indicate that smaller dimensional <a href="/wiki/Nanomaterials" title="Nanomaterials">nanomaterials</a> have higher surface area compared to 3D nanomaterials. Recently, <a href="/wiki/Two_dimensional_(2D)_nanomaterials" title="Two dimensional (2D) nanomaterials">two dimensional (2D) nanomaterials</a> are extensively investigated for <a href="/wiki/Electronics" title="Electronics">electronic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biomedical" class="mw-redirect" title="Biomedical">biomedical</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drug_delivery" title="Drug delivery">drug delivery</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biosensor" title="Biosensor">biosensor</a> applications.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tools_and_techniques">Tools and techniques</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:298px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AFMsetup.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/AFMsetup.jpg/296px-AFMsetup.jpg" width="296" height="234" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/AFMsetup.jpg/444px-AFMsetup.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/AFMsetup.jpg/592px-AFMsetup.jpg 2x" data-file-width="721" data-file-height="569" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AFMsetup.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Typical <a href="/wiki/Atomic_force_microscope" class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic force microscope">AFM</a> setup. A microfabricated <a href="/wiki/Cantilever" title="Cantilever">cantilever</a> with a sharp tip is deflected by features on a sample surface, much like in a <a href="/wiki/Phonograph" title="Phonograph">phonograph</a> but on a much smaller scale. A <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">laser</a> beam reflects off the backside of the cantilever into a set of <a href="/wiki/Photodetector" title="Photodetector">photodetectors</a>, allowing the deflection to be measured and assembled into an image of the surface.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There are several important modern developments. The <a href="/wiki/Atomic_force_microscope" class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic force microscope">atomic force microscope</a> (AFM) and the <a href="/wiki/Scanning_Tunneling_Microscope" class="mw-redirect" title="Scanning Tunneling Microscope">Scanning Tunneling Microscope</a> (STM) are two early versions of scanning probes that launched nanotechnology. There are other types of <a href="/wiki/Scanning_probe_microscopy" title="Scanning probe microscopy">scanning probe microscopy</a>. Although conceptually similar to the scanning <a href="/wiki/Confocal_microscope" class="mw-redirect" title="Confocal microscope">confocal microscope</a> developed by <a href="/wiki/Marvin_Minsky" title="Marvin Minsky">Marvin Minsky</a> in 1961 and the <a href="/wiki/Scanning_acoustic_microscope" title="Scanning acoustic microscope">scanning acoustic microscope</a> (SAM) developed by <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Quate" title="Calvin Quate">Calvin Quate</a> and coworkers in the 1970s, newer scanning probe microscopes have much higher resolution, since they are not limited by the wavelength of sound or light.</p>
<p>The tip of a scanning probe can also be used to manipulate nanostructures (a process called positional assembly). <a href="/wiki/Feature-oriented_scanning" title="Feature-oriented scanning">Feature-oriented scanning</a> methodology may be a promising way to implement these nanomanipulations in automatic mode.<sup id="cite_ref-feature2004_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feature2004-45">[45]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fospm2011_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fospm2011-46">[46]</a></sup> However, this is still a slow process because of low scanning velocity of the microscope.</p>
<p>Various techniques of nanolithography such as <a href="/wiki/Optical_lithography" class="mw-redirect" title="Optical lithography">optical lithography</a>, <a href="/wiki/X-ray_lithography" title="X-ray lithography">X-ray lithography</a> dip pen nanolithography, <a href="/wiki/Electron_beam_lithography" class="mw-redirect" title="Electron beam lithography">electron beam lithography</a> or <a href="/wiki/Nanoimprint_lithography" title="Nanoimprint lithography">nanoimprint lithography</a> were also developed. Lithography is a top-down fabrication technique where a bulk material is reduced in size to nanoscale pattern.</p>
<p>Another group of nanotechnological techniques include those used for fabrication of <a href="/wiki/Ion_track_technology_(track_etching)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ion track technology (track etching)">nanotubes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ion_track_technology_(track_replication)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ion track technology (track replication)">nanowires</a>, those used in semiconductor fabrication such as deep ultraviolet lithography, electron beam lithography, focused ion beam machining, nanoimprint lithography, atomic layer deposition, and molecular vapor deposition, and further including molecular self-assembly techniques such as those employing di-block copolymers. The precursors of these techniques preceded the nanotech era, and are extensions in the development of scientific advancements rather than techniques which were devised with the sole purpose of creating nanotechnology and which were results of nanotechnology research.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid26295171_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26295171-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>The top-down approach anticipates nanodevices that must be built piece by piece in stages, much as manufactured items are made. Scanning probe microscopy is an important technique both for characterization and synthesis of nanomaterials. Atomic force microscopes and scanning tunneling microscopes can be used to look at surfaces and to move atoms around. By designing different tips for these microscopes, they can be used for carving out structures on surfaces and to help guide self-assembling structures. By using, for example, feature-oriented scanning approach, atoms or molecules can be moved around on a surface with scanning probe microscopy techniques.<sup id="cite_ref-feature2004_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feature2004-45">[45]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fospm2011_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fospm2011-46">[46]</a></sup> At present, it is expensive and time-consuming for mass production but very suitable for laboratory experimentation.</p>
<p>In contrast, bottom-up techniques build or grow larger structures atom by atom or molecule by molecule. These techniques include chemical synthesis, <a href="/wiki/Self-assembly" title="Self-assembly">self-assembly</a> and positional assembly. <a href="/wiki/Dual_polarisation_interferometry" class="mw-redirect" title="Dual polarisation interferometry">Dual polarisation interferometry</a> is one tool suitable for characterisation of self assembled thin films. Another variation of the bottom-up approach is <a href="/wiki/Molecular_beam_epitaxy" title="Molecular beam epitaxy">molecular beam epitaxy</a> or MBE. Researchers at <a href="/wiki/Bell_Telephone_Laboratories" class="mw-redirect" title="Bell Telephone Laboratories">Bell Telephone Laboratories</a> like John R. Arthur. Alfred Y. Cho, and Art C. Gossard developed and implemented MBE as a research tool in the late 1960s and 1970s. Samples made by MBE were key to the discovery of the fractional quantum Hall effect for which the 1998 Nobel Prize in Physics was awarded. MBE allows scientists to lay down atomically precise layers of atoms and, in the process, build up complex structures. Important for research on semiconductors, MBE is also widely used to make samples and devices for the newly emerging field of <a href="/wiki/Spintronics" title="Spintronics">spintronics</a>.</p>
<p>However, new therapeutic products, based on responsive nanomaterials, such as the ultradeformable, stress-sensitive <a href="/wiki/Transfersome" title="Transfersome">Transfersome</a> vesicles, are under development and already approved for human use in some countries.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Threshold_formation_nowatermark.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Threshold_formation_nowatermark.gif/400px-Threshold_formation_nowatermark.gif" width="400" height="182" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Threshold_formation_nowatermark.gif/600px-Threshold_formation_nowatermark.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/43/Threshold_formation_nowatermark.gif 2x" data-file-width="722" data-file-height="328" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Threshold_formation_nowatermark.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
One of the major applications of nanotechnology is in the area of <a href="/wiki/Nanoelectronics" title="Nanoelectronics">nanoelectronics</a> with <a href="/wiki/MOSFET" title="MOSFET">MOSFET</a>'s being made of small <a href="/wiki/Nanowire" title="Nanowire">nanowires</a> ~10 nm in length. Here is a simulation of such a nanowire.</div>
</div>
</div>

</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_nanotechnology_applications" class="mw-redirect" title="List of nanotechnology applications">List of nanotechnology applications</a></div>
<p>As of August 21, 2008, the <a href="/wiki/Project_on_Emerging_Nanotechnologies" title="Project on Emerging Nanotechnologies">Project on Emerging Nanotechnologies</a> estimates that over 800 manufacturer-identified nanotech products are publicly available, with new ones hitting the market at a pace of 3–4 per week.<sup id="cite_ref-emergingnano_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emergingnano-13">[13]</a></sup> The project lists all of the products in a publicly accessible online database. Most applications are limited to the use of "first generation" passive nanomaterials which includes titanium dioxide in sunscreen, cosmetics, surface coatings,<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> and some food products; Carbon allotropes used to produce <a href="/wiki/Gecko_tape" class="mw-redirect" title="Gecko tape">gecko tape</a>; silver in food packaging, clothing, disinfectants and household appliances; zinc oxide in sunscreens and cosmetics, surface coatings, paints and outdoor furniture varnishes; and cerium oxide as a fuel catalyst.<sup id="cite_ref-americanelements_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-americanelements-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Further applications allow <a href="/wiki/Tennis_ball" title="Tennis ball">tennis balls</a> to last longer, <a href="/wiki/Golf_ball" title="Golf ball">golf balls</a> to fly straighter, and even <a href="/wiki/Bowling_ball" title="Bowling ball">bowling balls</a> to become more durable and have a harder surface. <a href="/wiki/Trouser" class="mw-redirect" title="Trouser">Trousers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Socks" class="mw-redirect" title="Socks">socks</a> have been infused with nanotechnology so that they will last longer and keep people cool in the summer. <a href="/wiki/Bandage" title="Bandage">Bandages</a> are being infused with silver nanoparticles to heal cuts faster.<sup id="cite_ref-nnin_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nnin-50">[50]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Video_game_console" title="Video game console">Video game consoles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Personal_computer" title="Personal computer">personal computers</a> may become cheaper, faster, and contain more memory thanks to nanotechnology.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Nanotechnology may have the ability to make existing medical applications cheaper and easier to use in places like the <a href="/wiki/General_practitioner" title="General practitioner">general practitioner</a>'s office and at home.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Cars are being manufactured with <a href="/wiki/Nanomaterial" class="mw-redirect" title="Nanomaterial">nanomaterials</a> so they may need fewer <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metals</a> and less <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuel</a> to operate in the future.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>Scientists are now turning to nanotechnology in an attempt to develop diesel engines with cleaner exhaust fumes. Platinum is currently used as the diesel engine <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalyst</a> in these engines. The catalyst is what cleans the exhaust fume particles. First a reduction catalyst is employed to take nitrogen atoms from NOx molecules in order to free oxygen. Next the oxidation catalyst oxidizes the hydrocarbons and carbon monoxide to form carbon dioxide and water.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Platinum is used in both the reduction and the oxidation catalysts.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Using platinum though, is inefficient in that it is expensive and unsustainable. Danish company InnovationsFonden invested DKK 15 million in a search for new catalyst substitutes using nanotechnology. The goal of the project, launched in the autumn of 2014, is to maximize surface area and minimize the amount of material required. Objects tend to minimize their surface energy; two drops of water, for example, will join to form one drop and decrease surface area. If the catalyst's surface area that is exposed to the exhaust fumes is maximized, efficiency of the catalyst is maximized. The team working on this project aims to create nanoparticles that will not merge. Every time the surface is optimized, material is saved. Thus, creating these nanoparticles will increase the effectiveness of the resulting diesel engine catalyst—in turn leading to cleaner exhaust fumes—and will decrease cost. If successful, the team hopes to reduce platinum use by 25%.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nanotechnology also has a prominent role in the fast developing field of <a href="/wiki/Tissue_Engineering" class="mw-redirect" title="Tissue Engineering">Tissue Engineering</a>. When designing scaffolds, researchers attempt to the mimic the nanoscale features of a <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">Cell</a>'s microenvironment to direct its differentiation down a suitable lineage.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> For example, when creating scaffolds to support the growth of bone, researchers may mimic <a href="/wiki/Osteoclast" title="Osteoclast">osteoclast</a> <a href="/w/index.php?title=Resorption_pits&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Resorption pits (page does not exist)">resorption pits</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>Researchers have successfully used <a href="/wiki/DNA_origami" title="DNA origami">DNA origami</a>-based nanobots capable of carrying out logic functions to achieve targeted drug delivery in cockroaches. It is said that the computational power of these nanobots can be scaled up to that of a <a href="/wiki/Commodore_64" title="Commodore 64">Commodore 64</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Implications">Implications</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Implications_of_nanotechnology" class="mw-redirect" title="Implications of nanotechnology">Implications of nanotechnology</a></div>
<p>An area of concern is the effect that industrial-scale manufacturing and use of nanomaterials would have on human health and the environment, as suggested by <a href="/wiki/Nanotoxicology" title="Nanotoxicology">nanotoxicology</a> research. For these reasons, some groups advocate that nanotechnology be regulated by governments. Others counter that overregulation would stifle scientific research and the development of beneficial innovations. <a href="/wiki/Public_health" title="Public health">Public health</a> research agencies, such as the <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_for_Occupational_Safety_and_Health" title="National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health">National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health</a> are actively conducting research on potential health effects stemming from exposures to nanoparticles.<sup id="cite_ref-niosh_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-niosh-60">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nioshnano_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nioshnano-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some nanoparticle products may have <a href="/wiki/Unintended_consequences" title="Unintended consequences">unintended consequences</a>. Researchers have discovered that <a href="/wiki/Bacteriostatic" class="mw-redirect" title="Bacteriostatic">bacteriostatic</a> silver nanoparticles used in socks to reduce foot odor are being released in the wash.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> These particles are then flushed into the waste water stream and may destroy bacteria which are critical components of natural ecosystems, farms, and waste treatment processes.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>Public deliberations on <a href="/wiki/Risk_perception" title="Risk perception">risk perception</a> in the US and UK carried out by the Center for Nanotechnology in Society found that participants were more positive about nanotechnologies for energy applications than for health applications, with health applications raising moral and ethical dilemmas such as cost and availability.<sup id="cite_ref-harthorn_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harthorn-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>Experts, including director of the Woodrow Wilson Center's Project on Emerging Nanotechnologies David Rejeski, have testified<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> that successful commercialization depends on adequate oversight, risk research strategy, and public engagement. <a href="/wiki/Berkeley,_California" title="Berkeley, California">Berkeley, California</a> is currently the only city in the United States to regulate nanotechnology;<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cambridge,_Massachusetts" title="Cambridge, Massachusetts">Cambridge, Massachusetts</a> in 2008 considered enacting a similar law,<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> but ultimately rejected it.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Relevant for both research on and application of nanotechnologies, the <a href="/wiki/Insurability" title="Insurability">insurability</a> of nanotechnology is contested.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Without state <a href="/wiki/Regulation_of_nanotechnology" title="Regulation of nanotechnology">regulation of nanotechnology</a>, the availability of private insurance for potential damages is seen as necessary to ensure that burdens are not socialised implicitly.</p>

<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NIOSH_Nano_Research_-_Engineering_Controls_for_Nanomaterial_Production_and_Handling_Processes.webm" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A video on the health and safety implications of nanotechnology</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Nanotoxicology" title="Nanotoxicology">Nanotoxicology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pollution_from_nanomaterials" title="Pollution from nanomaterials">Pollution from nanomaterials</a></div>
<p>Nanofibers are used in several areas and in different products, in everything from aircraft wings to tennis rackets. Inhaling airborne nanoparticles and nanofibers may lead to a number of <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Pulmonary disease">pulmonary diseases</a>, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Fibrosis" title="Fibrosis">fibrosis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Researchers have found that when rats breathed in nanoparticles, the particles settled in the brain and lungs, which led to significant increases in biomarkers for inflammation and stress response<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> and that nanoparticles induce skin aging through oxidative stress in hairless mice.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>A two-year study at UCLA's School of Public Health found lab mice consuming nano-titanium dioxide showed DNA and chromosome damage to a degree "linked to all the big killers of man, namely cancer, heart disease, neurological disease and aging".<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>A major study published more recently in <a href="/wiki/Nature_Nanotechnology" title="Nature Nanotechnology">Nature Nanotechnology</a> suggests some forms of carbon nanotubes – a poster child for the "nanotechnology revolution" – could be as harmful as <a href="/wiki/Asbestos" title="Asbestos">asbestos</a> if inhaled in sufficient quantities. <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Seaton" title="Anthony Seaton">Anthony Seaton</a> of the Institute of Occupational Medicine in Edinburgh, Scotland, who contributed to the article on <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nanotube" title="Carbon nanotube">carbon nanotubes</a> said "We know that some of them probably have the potential to cause mesothelioma. So those sorts of materials need to be handled very carefully."<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> In the absence of specific regulation forthcoming from governments, Paull and Lyons (2008) have called for an exclusion of engineered nanoparticles in food.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> A newspaper article reports that workers in a paint factory developed serious lung disease and nanoparticles were found in their lungs.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bbcfibre00_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcfibre00-78">[78]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulation">Regulation</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Regulation_of_nanotechnology" title="Regulation of nanotechnology">Regulation of nanotechnology</a></div>
<p>Calls for tighter regulation of nanotechnology have occurred alongside a growing debate related to the human health and safety risks of nanotechnology.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> There is significant debate about who is responsible for the regulation of nanotechnology. Some regulatory agencies currently cover some nanotechnology products and processes (to varying degrees) – by "bolting on" nanotechnology to existing regulations – there are clear gaps in these regimes.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Davies (2008) has proposed a regulatory road map describing steps to deal with these shortcomings.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>Stakeholders concerned by the lack of a regulatory framework to assess and control risks associated with the release of nanoparticles and nanotubes have drawn parallels with <a href="/wiki/Bovine_spongiform_encephalopathy" title="Bovine spongiform encephalopathy">bovine spongiform encephalopathy</a> ("mad cow" disease), <a href="/wiki/Thalidomide" title="Thalidomide">thalidomide</a>, genetically modified food,<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> nuclear energy, reproductive technologies, biotechnology, and <a href="/wiki/Asbestosis" title="Asbestosis">asbestosis</a>. Dr. Andrew Maynard, chief science advisor to the Woodrow Wilson Center's Project on Emerging Nanotechnologies, concludes that there is insufficient funding for human health and safety research, and as a result there is currently limited understanding of the human health and safety risks associated with nanotechnology.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> As a result, some academics have called for stricter application of the <a href="/wiki/Precautionary_principle" title="Precautionary principle">precautionary principle</a>, with delayed marketing approval, enhanced labelling and additional safety data development requirements in relation to certain forms of nanotechnology.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Royal Society report<sup id="cite_ref-royalsociety_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-royalsociety-10">[10]</a></sup> identified a risk of nanoparticles or nanotubes being released during disposal, destruction and recycling, and recommended that "manufacturers of products that fall under extended producer responsibility regimes such as end-of-life regulations publish procedures outlining how these materials will be managed to minimize possible human and environmental exposure" (p. xiii).</p>
<p>The Center for Nanotechnology in Society has found that people respond to nanotechnologies differently, depending on application – with participants in <a href="/wiki/Deliberations" class="mw-redirect" title="Deliberations">public deliberations</a> more positive about nanotechnologies for energy than health applications – suggesting that any public calls for nano regulations may differ by technology sector.<sup id="cite_ref-harthorn_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harthorn-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
